Japan: Context (1920-1930)

Governmental Regulations on Religion and Legal Cases

Country: Japan

Historical Period: 1920-1930

Governmental Regulations on Religion

  1. Please describe important governmental regulations on religion in this decade, including the Constitution‚Äôs clauses on or relevant to religion, laws on religion, laws pertinent to religion (such as on education, on military service, on public security, on civic organizations, etc.) and type your answer in the ‚ÄúYour Answers‚Äù section below.

    (Please mark the checkboxes upon completing the respective instructions):

    Please follow the rubric to organize your answer and check the box at the end of each instruction.

    1. Summary of restrictive regulations on religion in this decade (500-1,000 words). ‚òê

    2. List the names of the particular or pertinent laws in CJK (Chinese/Japanese/Korean) and English. ‚òê

    3. Bibliographical sources (in CJK and English). ‚òê

    Your Answer:

    A.

    During this period, there were still no specific restrictions on religion. Still, the scope of application of the less majesty(‰∏çÊï¨ÁΩ™, 1880Ôºâand police punishment (Ë≠¶ÂØüÁäØÂá¶ÁΩ∞‰ª§, 1908) was expanded to include policing and suppression of faith. In particular, the police punishment decree was easy to invoke, and new religions that were regarded by the police authorities as ‚Äúlicentiousness‚Äù were often restricted from expanding their organizations through frequent activities. On the other hand, the crime of impiety was first applied in the first Oomoto suppression. However, caution was required in its invocation to control the social spread and large-scale growth of new religions with doctrines that conflicted with the national constitution. (Sakamoto, 477)„ÄÄFurthermore, in 1925, Peace Preservation Law was enacted. This was intended to punish the organization and the joining of associations whose purpose was to transform the state and deny the private property system. In practice, however, it functioned broadly as a sweeping crackdown on freedom of expression.(18)

    B.

    1925 Peace Preservation Law ÔºàÊ≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï, Chian iji h≈ç) „Éª It was enacted to crack down on socialist and labor movements. Under this law, it prohibited any social activities which rejected the emperor-centered political system and private property. In 1928, the death penalty was added as a maximum sentence, and the law was also manipulated to suppress the labor disputes, the democratic protests, and religious organizations. During this period, there were still no specific restrictions on religion. Still, the scope of application of the decree of impiety and police punishment was expanded to include policing and suppression of faith.

    1925 Peace Preservation Law ÔºàÊ≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï, Chian iji h≈ç)

    Á¨¨ÂÖ≠Ê¢ù„ÄÄÂâç‰∫îÊ¢ù„ÉéÁΩ™„É≤ÁäØ„Ç∑„Çø„É´ËÄÖËá™È¶ñ„Ç∑„Çø„É´„Éà„Ç≠„ÉèÂÖ∂„ÉéÂàë„É≤Ê∏õËºïÂèà„ÉèÂÖçÈô§„Çπ

    Á¨¨‰∏ÉÊ¢ù„ÄÄÊú¨Ê≥ï„Éè‰Ωï‰∫∫„É≤Âïè„Éè„ÇπÊú¨Ê≥ïÊñΩË°åÂçÄÂüüÂ§ñ„ÉãÊñº„ÉÜÁΩ™„É≤ÁäØ„Ç∑„Çø„É´ËÄÖ„Éã‰∫¶‰πã„É≤ÈÅ©Áî®„Çπ

    ÈôÑÂâá

    Peace Preservation Law, 1925

    1. Anyone who forms, or knowingly participates in, groups whose goal is to deny the system of private property or to change our national essence shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to ten years. Anyone who attempts to commit this crime also will be punished.

    2. Anyone who engages in discussions intended to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to seven years.

    3. Anyone who instigates actions by others intended to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to seven years.

    4. Anyone intending to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One who instigates crimes resulting in riots or violence that cause damage to property, bodily injury, or destruction of life, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to ten years.

    5. Anyone who, intending to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One or of the crimes described in the three previous clauses, gives money, goods, or other financial benefits to others, or makes promises of such for that same purpose, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to five years. Anyone who knowingly demands or receives such remuneration, or makes a commitment for such remuneration, shall be given the same punishment.

    6. Anyone guilty of the offenses in the five preceding articles who surrenders voluntarily to the police shall receive a lighter sentence or be exonerated.

    7. This law shall apply to anyone who commits these crimes, even if that person lives outside the regions where the law has jurisdiction.

    More detail about Peace Preservation Law, 1925

    C.

    • Draft law in Tokyo Asahi Shimbun, February 13, 1925, in Uchikawa Yoshimi and Matsushima Eiichi, eds. Taish≈ç ny≈´su jiten (Encyclopedia of Taish≈ç news). Vol. 7. Tokyo: Mainichi Komiyunkƒìshiyon Shuppanbu, 1989), 405. James L. Huffman, trans.

    Social Hostility toward Christianity

    1. Please describe important non-governmental hostility toward Christianity in this decade, including motivated by a particular religion, nationalism, economic and other reasons and type your answer in the ‚ÄúYour Answers‚Äù section below..

      (Please mark the checkboxes upon completing the respective instructions):

      Please follow the rubric to organize your answer and check the box at the end of each instruction.

      1. Summary of the social hostility in this decade, including conflicts and cooperation of multiple religions (500-1,000 words). ‚òê

      2. Bibliographical sources in CJK and English. ‚òê

      Your Answer:

      After the death of founder Nao Deguchi in 1918, Oninsaburo Deguchi became the center of the organization, and from around that time onward, it expanded throughout Japan. In addition, people related to the navy and the army joined the Omoto, and their activities attracted the attention of the judicial authorities. In 1919, investigations began into the Imperial Way of Oomoto-kyo (495) The Kyoto Prefectural Police sent in police to gather information, and on February 11, 1921, Onizaburo and three others were arrested. At the trial, the charges against Deguchi Oninsaburo and Asano Kazaburo included violations of the Penal Code for disrespect and the Newspaper Law. The charges against Deguchi and Asano were enumerated. In the section on disrespect, it was stated that they had committed an act of gross desecration and disrespect of the Emperor‚Äôs dignity by writing articles in a newspaper published by the Omoto that delusionally commented on the Emperor‚Äôs actions, violated the wisdom of the Emperor, desecrated his dignity, and ignored his right to rule. Furthermore, publishing such an article violated the Newspaper Law. (496)

      1928 First Suppression of Honmichi(„Åª„Çì„Åø„Å°Á¨¨‰∏ÄÊ¨°ÂºæÂúß)

      This incident, known as the First Tenrikyo Research Association Incident, became an issue of disrespect. The Tenri Kenkyukai, which was started by Aijiro Onishi, a Tenrikyo believer, based on his interpretation, became an issue of disrespect to the emperor, and arrests continued from April 8 to August 1928. Almost 180 people were prosecuted, and 287 were exempted from prosecution. (496).

      One opportunity to consider this period regarding the relationship between Japan and U.S. Christianity is the enactment of the Immigration Act of 1924 and also anti-American sentiment among the Japanese. The law went into effect on July 1, 1924, but in June, a declaration opposing the U.S. immigration law was issued by the Japanese Christian League.When the Immigration Act of 1924 was proposed, Some of Christians in Tokyo gathered at Shigenobu Okuma‚Äôs house and sent a telegram to the President of the United States to stop this Act, pointing out that it would harm US-Japan relations(Sunakawa, 1965, vol.2, 202).

      After the Immigrant Act passage, Christians condemned it as the most severe violation of the spirit of Christianity, and on June 27, they issued a resolution jointly signed by themÔºàSunakawa, 1965,vol.2, 203)

      .

      Japan Christian Volunteers‚Äô Resolution Against U.S. the Immigration Act of 1924

      1. In the spirit of our previous efforts to prevent such legislation, we Christians unite in urging the American people to reflect on the situation and to take every opportunity to resolve it thoroughly.

      2. We Christians, who believe in racial equality for all humankind, look forward to working together with Christians worldwide to realize this ideal internationally and make further efforts in the future.„ÄÄ

      3. We Christian believers know the seriousness of our responsibility to the current times. To accomplish our mission, we expect you to promote and complete the independence of Christian churches and other Christian organizations, which has been our desire for many years.

      After the passage of this Immigrant Act, Inazo Nitobe, who had studied in the United States and was a pro-American Christian, stated that he would ‚Äúnever step on American soil again‚Äù (Sawada, 2019, 133). Many other Christians were disappointed by the passage of the Act and were concerned about the deterioration of relations with the United States.

      On the other hand, in response to such anti-American sentiment in Japan, activities were developed to solve the tension between the two countries through private cultural exchanges. It was called Friendship Dolls(Japanese friendship dolls (ÂèãÊÉÖ‰∫∫ÂΩ¢, y≈´j≈ç ningy≈ç), or Japanese ambassador dolls, and the American blue-eyed dolls (Èùí„ÅÑÁõÆ„ÅÆ‰∫∫ÂΩ¢, aoi me no ningy≈ç). About 12,000 dolls were donated from the United States in time for the Japanese Doll Festival. The movement was planned by Sydney Lewis Gulick (1860-1945), a missionary of the American Board who worked in Japan for many years. In 1925 the Committee of World Friendship among Children (CWFC) was formed. The first project of the CWFC was a goodwill activity through dolls during the Doll Festival on March 3, 1927 (Kohiyama, 2005, 57). On the Japanese side, the Ministry of Education and Foreign Affairs distributed the dolls, and the Shibusawa Foundation covered most of the expenses after Eiichi Shibusawa endorsed the project. On the other hand, 58 Japanese dolls were presented to the United States in November as ‚Äúreply dolls‚Äù led by Shibusawa. They were donated to museums and art galleries throughout the United States.IIt was mentioned that many Christian women and their traditions behind the doll exchange.(Kohiyama, 2005, 65) Since the 19th century, many American women have engaged in overseas missionary work. As an extension, those who had previously participated in such missions created and prepared doll clothing (Kohiyama, 2005,61). Thus, it is shown that American Christian women supported this activity. On the other hand, Gulick and the CWFC were careful not to indicate too much that their activities were religious. They thought that it was essential to appeal to secular groups to receive support from the wider public (Kohiyama, 2005,62). Therefore, prioritizing the dolls occurred over appeals to ease US-Japan relations, such as ‚Äúfriendship,‚Äù rather than religious connotations(Kohiyama, 2005, 63-64). Thus, the project forgot about American women and the religion behind it. It was carried out to improve US-Japan relations and benefit the country, which was the concern of Japanese male leaders(Kohiyama, 2005, 66) . Furthermore, the project was also implemented in the colonies and used to reinforce Japanese colonial policies.

      • Kohiyama, Rui. To Clear up a Cloud Hanging on the Pacific Ocean: The 1927 Japan-U.S. Doll Exchange, The Japanese Journal of American Studies, No. 16, 2005, 55-80.

        „ÄÄ„Åï„Çâ„Å´„ÄÅ‰∫î„Ç´ÂõΩÊù°Á¥ÑÔºà„ÉØ„Ç∑„É≥„Éà„É≥Êµ∑ËªçËªçÁ∏ÆÊù°Á¥ÑÔºâ„Åß„ÅØ„ÄÅÂêÑÂõΩ„ÅÆ‰∏ªÂäõËâ¶ÔºàÊà¶Ëâ¶„Å®Á©∫ÊØçÔºâÂª∫ÈÄ†„Çí10Âπ¥ÈñìÂÅúÊ≠¢„Åó„ÄÅÂêÑÂõΩ„ÅÆ‰øùÊúâÊØîÁéá„Çí„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„Å®„Ç¢„É°„É™„Ç´„ÅåÂêÑÔºï„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨3„ÄÅ„Éï„É©„É≥„Çπ„Å®„Ç§„Çø„É™„Ç¢„ÅåÂêÑ1.67„Å®Âèñ„ÇäÊ±∫„ÇÅ„ÅüÊù°Á¥Ñ„Åß„ÅÇ„Å£„Åü„ÄÇÊó•Êú¨ÂÅ¥„ÅØË≤°ÊîøË≤†ÊãÖ„ÇÑÂØæÁ±≥ÂçîË™ø„ÅÆË¶≥ÁÇπ„Åã„Çâ„Åì„ÅÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„ÇíÂèóË´æ„Åó„ÄÅ„Åù„ÅÆ‰ª£„Çè„Çä„Å´„ÄÅÂ§™Âπ≥Ê¥ã„ÅÆÂ≥∂„ÄÖ„Å´„Åä„Åë„ÇãÊµ∑ËªçÂü∫Âú∞„ÅÆÂº∑ÂåñÁ¶ÅÊ≠¢„ÇíÊèêÊ°à„Åó„ÄÅÁ¨¨19Êù°„Å®„Åó„Å¶ÊâøË™ç„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„Å´„Åä„ÅÑ„Å¶ÊúÄ„ÇÇÂ§ö„Åè„ÅÆËªçËâ¶„ÇíÂªÉÊ£Ñ„Åó„Åü„ÅÆ„ÅØ„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅ„Åì„ÅÆ„Åì„Å®„ÇÇ„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„ÅÆÂΩ±ÈüøÂäõ„ÅÆ‰Ωé‰∏ã„Å´„Å§„Å™„Åå„Å£„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊµ∑ËªçËªçÁ∏ÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„Å´„Çà„ÇäÁ¨¨‰∏ÄÊ¨°‰∏ñÁïåÂ§ßÊà¶„Åã„ÇâÁ∂ö„ÅèÊà¶Ëâ¶Âª∫ÈÄ†„ÅÆÁ´∂‰∫â„ÅØ‰∏ÄÊôÇÁöÑ„Å´Êäë„Åà„Çâ„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ(Ryuji Hattori, 2016, 171 )

        „ÄêChina„Äë

        E.

Social Hostility toward Christianity

A.

During this period, there were still no specific restrictions on religion. Still, the scope of application of the less majesty(‰∏çÊï¨ÁΩ™, 1880Ôºâand police punishment (Ë≠¶ÂØüÁäØÂá¶ÁΩ∞‰ª§, 1908) was expanded to include policing and suppression of faith. In particular, the police punishment decree was easy to invoke, and new religions that were regarded by the police authorities as ‚Äúlicentiousness‚Äù were often restricted from expanding their organizations through frequent activities. On the other hand, the crime of impiety was first applied in the first Oomoto suppression. However, caution was required in its invocation to control the social spread and large-scale growth of new religions with doctrines that conflicted with the national constitution. (Sakamoto, 477)„ÄÄFurthermore, in 1925, Peace Preservation Law was enacted. This was intended to punish the organization and the joining of associations whose purpose was to transform the state and deny the private property system. In practice, however, it functioned broadly as a sweeping crackdown on freedom of expression.(18)

B.

1925 Peace Preservation Law ÔºàÊ≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï, Chian iji h≈ç) „Éª It was enacted to crack down on socialist and labor movements. Under this law, it prohibited any social activities which rejected the emperor-centered political system and private property. In 1928, the death penalty was added as a maximum sentence, and the law was also manipulated to suppress the labor disputes, the democratic protests, and religious organizations. During this period, there were still no specific restrictions on religion. Still, the scope of application of the decree of impiety and police punishment was expanded to include policing and suppression of faith.

Peace Preservation Law, 1925

1. Anyone who forms, or knowingly participates in, groups whose goal is to deny the system of private property or to change our national essence shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to ten years. Anyone who attempts to commit this crime also will be punished.

2. Anyone who engages in discussions intended to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to seven years.

3. Anyone who instigates actions by others intended to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to seven years.

4. Anyone intending to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One who instigates crimes resulting in riots or violence that cause damage to property, bodily injury, or destruction of life, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to ten years.

5. Anyone who, intending to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One or of the crimes described in the three previous clauses, gives money, goods, or other financial benefits to others, or makes promises of such for that same purpose, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to five years. Anyone who knowingly demands or receives such remuneration, or makes a commitment for such remuneration, shall be given the same punishment.

6. Anyone guilty of the offenses in the five preceding articles who surrenders voluntarily to the police shall receive a lighter sentence or be exonerated.

7. This law shall apply to anyone who commits these crimes, even if that person lives outside the regions where the law has jurisdiction.

More detail about Peace Preservation Law, 1925

Geopolitical Dynamics and International Relationships

Country: Japan

Historical Period: 1920-1930

Governmental Regulations on Religion

  1. Please describe important governmental regulations on religion in this decade, including the Constitution‚Äôs clauses on or relevant to religion, laws on religion, laws pertinent to religion (such as on education, on military service, on public security, on civic organizations, etc.) and type your answer in the ‚ÄúYour Answers‚Äù section below.

    (Please mark the checkboxes upon completing the respective instructions):

    Please follow the rubric to organize your answer and check the box at the end of each instruction.

    1. Summary of restrictive regulations on religion in this decade (500-1,000 words). ‚òê

    2. List the names of the particular or pertinent laws in CJK (Chinese/Japanese/Korean) and English. ‚òê

    3. Bibliographical sources (in CJK and English). ‚òê

    Your Answer:

    A.

    During this period, there were still no specific restrictions on religion. Still, the scope of application of the less majesty(‰∏çÊï¨ÁΩ™, 1880Ôºâand police punishment (Ë≠¶ÂØüÁäØÂá¶ÁΩ∞‰ª§, 1908) was expanded to include policing and suppression of faith. In particular, the police punishment decree was easy to invoke, and new religions that were regarded by the police authorities as ‚Äúlicentiousness‚Äù were often restricted from expanding their organizations through frequent activities. On the other hand, the crime of impiety was first applied in the first Oomoto suppression. However, caution was required in its invocation to control the social spread and large-scale growth of new religions with doctrines that conflicted with the national constitution. (Sakamoto, 477)„ÄÄFurthermore, in 1925, Peace Preservation Law was enacted. This was intended to punish the organization and the joining of associations whose purpose was to transform the state and deny the private property system. In practice, however, it functioned broadly as a sweeping crackdown on freedom of expression.(18)

    B.

    1925 Peace Preservation Law ÔºàÊ≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï, Chian iji h≈ç) „Éª It was enacted to crack down on socialist and labor movements. Under this law, it prohibited any social activities which rejected the emperor-centered political system and private property. In 1928, the death penalty was added as a maximum sentence, and the law was also manipulated to suppress the labor disputes, the democratic protests, and religious organizations. During this period, there were still no specific restrictions on religion. Still, the scope of application of the decree of impiety and police punishment was expanded to include policing and suppression of faith.

    ÈôÑÂâá

    Peace Preservation Law, 1925

    1. Anyone who forms, or knowingly participates in, groups whose goal is to deny the system of private property or to change our national essence shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to ten years. Anyone who attempts to commit this crime also will be punished.

    2. Anyone who engages in discussions intended to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to seven years.

    3. Anyone who instigates actions by others intended to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to seven years.

    4. Anyone intending to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One who instigates crimes resulting in riots or violence that cause damage to property, bodily injury, or destruction of life, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to ten years.

    5. Anyone who, intending to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One or of the crimes described in the three previous clauses, gives money, goods, or other financial benefits to others, or makes promises of such for that same purpose, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to five years. Anyone who knowingly demands or receives such remuneration, or makes a commitment for such remuneration, shall be given the same punishment.

    6. Anyone guilty of the offenses in the five preceding articles who surrenders voluntarily to the police shall receive a lighter sentence or be exonerated.

    7. This law shall apply to anyone who commits these crimes, even if that person lives outside the regions where the law has jurisdiction.

    More detail about Peace Preservation Law, 1925

    Social Hostility toward Christianity

    1. Please describe important non-governmental hostility toward Christianity in this decade, including motivated by a particular religion, nationalism, economic and other reasons and type your answer in the ‚ÄúYour Answers‚Äù section below..

      (Please mark the checkboxes upon completing the respective instructions):

      Please follow the rubric to organize your answer and check the box at the end of each instruction.

      1. Summary of the social hostility in this decade, including conflicts and cooperation of multiple religions (500-1,000 words). ‚òê

      2. Bibliographical sources in CJK and English. ‚òê

      Your Answer:

      After the death of founder Nao Deguchi in 1918, Oninsaburo Deguchi became the center of the organization, and from around that time onward, it expanded throughout Japan. In addition, people related to the navy and the army joined the Omoto, and their activities attracted the attention of the judicial authorities. In 1919, investigations began into the Imperial Way of Oomoto-kyo (495) The Kyoto Prefectural Police sent in police to gather information, and on February 11, 1921, Onizaburo and three others were arrested. At the trial, the charges against Deguchi Oninsaburo and Asano Kazaburo included violations of the Penal Code for disrespect and the Newspaper Law. The charges against Deguchi and Asano were enumerated. In the section on disrespect, it was stated that they had committed an act of gross desecration and disrespect of the Emperor‚Äôs dignity by writing articles in a newspaper published by the Omoto that delusionally commented on the Emperor‚Äôs actions, violated the wisdom of the Emperor, desecrated his dignity, and ignored his right to rule. Furthermore, publishing such an article violated the Newspaper Law. (496)

      1928 First Suppression of Honmichi(„Åª„Çì„Åø„Å°Á¨¨‰∏ÄÊ¨°ÂºæÂúß)

      This incident, known as the First Tenrikyo Research Association Incident, became an issue of disrespect. The Tenri Kenkyukai, which was started by Aijiro Onishi, a Tenrikyo believer, based on his interpretation, became an issue of disrespect to the emperor, and arrests continued from April 8 to August 1928. Almost 180 people were prosecuted, and 287 were exempted from prosecution. (496).

      One opportunity to consider this period regarding the relationship between Japan and U.S. Christianity is the enactment of the Immigration Act of 1924 and also anti-American sentiment among the Japanese. The law went into effect on July 1, 1924, but in June, a declaration opposing the U.S. immigration law was issued by the Japanese Christian League.When the Immigration Act of 1924 was proposed, Some of Christians in Tokyo gathered at Shigenobu Okuma‚Äôs house and sent a telegram to the President of the United States to stop this Act, pointing out that it would harm US-Japan relations(Sunakawa, 1965, vol.2, 202).

      After the Immigrant Act passage, Christians condemned it as the most severe violation of the spirit of Christianity, and on June 27, they issued a resolution jointly signed by themÔºàSunakawa, 1965,vol.2, 203)

      .

      Japan Christian Volunteers‚Äô Resolution Against U.S. the Immigration Act of 1924

      1. In the spirit of our previous efforts to prevent such legislation, we Christians unite in urging the American people to reflect on the situation and to take every opportunity to resolve it thoroughly.

      2. We Christians, who believe in racial equality for all humankind, look forward to working together with Christians worldwide to realize this ideal internationally and make further efforts in the future.„ÄÄ

      3. We Christian believers know the seriousness of our responsibility to the current times. To accomplish our mission, we expect you to promote and complete the independence of Christian churches and other Christian organizations, which has been our desire for many years.

      After the passage of this Immigrant Act, Inazo Nitobe, who had studied in the United States and was a pro-American Christian, stated that he would ‚Äúnever step on American soil again‚Äù (Sawada, 2019, 133). Many other Christians were disappointed by the passage of the Act and were concerned about the deterioration of relations with the United States.

      On the other hand, in response to such anti-American sentiment in Japan, activities were developed to solve the tension between the two countries through private cultural exchanges. It was called Friendship Dolls(Japanese friendship dolls (ÂèãÊÉÖ‰∫∫ÂΩ¢, y≈´j≈ç ningy≈ç), or Japanese ambassador dolls, and the American blue-eyed dolls (Èùí„ÅÑÁõÆ„ÅÆ‰∫∫ÂΩ¢, aoi me no ningy≈ç). About 12,000 dolls were donated from the United States in time for the Japanese Doll Festival. The movement was planned by Sydney Lewis Gulick (1860-1945), a missionary of the American Board who worked in Japan for many years. In 1925 the Committee of World Friendship among Children (CWFC) was formed. The first project of the CWFC was a goodwill activity through dolls during the Doll Festival on March 3, 1927 (Kohiyama, 2005, 57). On the Japanese side, the Ministry of Education and Foreign Affairs distributed the dolls, and the Shibusawa Foundation covered most of the expenses after Eiichi Shibusawa endorsed the project. On the other hand, 58 Japanese dolls were presented to the United States in November as ‚Äúreply dolls‚Äù led by Shibusawa. They were donated to museums and art galleries throughout the United States.IIt was mentioned that many Christian women and their traditions behind the doll exchange.(Kohiyama, 2005, 65) Since the 19th century, many American women have engaged in overseas missionary work. As an extension, those who had previously participated in such missions created and prepared doll clothing (Kohiyama, 2005,61). Thus, it is shown that American Christian women supported this activity. On the other hand, Gulick and the CWFC were careful not to indicate too much that their activities were religious. They thought that it was essential to appeal to secular groups to receive support from the wider public (Kohiyama, 2005,62). Therefore, prioritizing the dolls occurred over appeals to ease US-Japan relations, such as ‚Äúfriendship,‚Äù rather than religious connotations(Kohiyama, 2005, 63-64). Thus, the project forgot about American women and the religion behind it. It was carried out to improve US-Japan relations and benefit the country, which was the concern of Japanese male leaders(Kohiyama, 2005, 66) . Furthermore, the project was also implemented in the colonies and used to reinforce Japanese colonial policies.

        „ÄÄ„Åï„Çâ„Å´„ÄÅ‰∫î„Ç´ÂõΩÊù°Á¥ÑÔºà„ÉØ„Ç∑„É≥„Éà„É≥Êµ∑ËªçËªçÁ∏ÆÊù°Á¥ÑÔºâ„Åß„ÅØ„ÄÅÂêÑÂõΩ„ÅÆ‰∏ªÂäõËâ¶ÔºàÊà¶Ëâ¶„Å®Á©∫ÊØçÔºâÂª∫ÈÄ†„Çí10Âπ¥ÈñìÂÅúÊ≠¢„Åó„ÄÅÂêÑÂõΩ„ÅÆ‰øùÊúâÊØîÁéá„Çí„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„Å®„Ç¢„É°„É™„Ç´„ÅåÂêÑÔºï„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨3„ÄÅ„Éï„É©„É≥„Çπ„Å®„Ç§„Çø„É™„Ç¢„ÅåÂêÑ1.67„Å®Âèñ„ÇäÊ±∫„ÇÅ„ÅüÊù°Á¥Ñ„Åß„ÅÇ„Å£„Åü„ÄÇÊó•Êú¨ÂÅ¥„ÅØË≤°ÊîøË≤†ÊãÖ„ÇÑÂØæÁ±≥ÂçîË™ø„ÅÆË¶≥ÁÇπ„Åã„Çâ„Åì„ÅÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„ÇíÂèóË´æ„Åó„ÄÅ„Åù„ÅÆ‰ª£„Çè„Çä„Å´„ÄÅÂ§™Âπ≥Ê¥ã„ÅÆÂ≥∂„ÄÖ„Å´„Åä„Åë„ÇãÊµ∑ËªçÂü∫Âú∞„ÅÆÂº∑ÂåñÁ¶ÅÊ≠¢„ÇíÊèêÊ°à„Åó„ÄÅÁ¨¨19Êù°„Å®„Åó„Å¶ÊâøË™ç„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„Å´„Åä„ÅÑ„Å¶ÊúÄ„ÇÇÂ§ö„Åè„ÅÆËªçËâ¶„ÇíÂªÉÊ£Ñ„Åó„Åü„ÅÆ„ÅØ„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅ„Åì„ÅÆ„Åì„Å®„ÇÇ„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„ÅÆÂΩ±ÈüøÂäõ„ÅÆ‰Ωé‰∏ã„Å´„Å§„Å™„Åå„Å£„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊµ∑ËªçËªçÁ∏ÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„Å´„Çà„ÇäÁ¨¨‰∏ÄÊ¨°‰∏ñÁïåÂ§ßÊà¶„Åã„ÇâÁ∂ö„ÅèÊà¶Ëâ¶Âª∫ÈÄ†„ÅÆÁ´∂‰∫â„ÅØ‰∏ÄÊôÇÁöÑ„Å´Êäë„Åà„Çâ„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ(Ryuji Hattori, 2016, 171 )
        „ÄÄÊúÄÂæå„Å´„ÄÅ1922Âπ¥ÔºíÊúà„Å´ÂÖ®ÂèÇÂä†ÂõΩ„Å´„Çà„ÇãÔºô„Ç´ÂõΩÊù°Á¥Ñ„ÅåÁ∑†Áµê„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„Çå„ÅØ‰∏≠ÂõΩ„Å´Èñ¢„Åô„ÇãÊù°Á¥Ñ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅ‰∏≠ÂõΩ„ÅÆ‰∏ªÊ®©„ÅÆÂ∞äÈáç„ÄÅÈ†òÂúü‰øùÂÖ®„ÄÅÈñÄÊà∏ÈñãÊîæ„Å®Ê©ü‰ºöÂùáÁ≠â„ÅåÁ¢∫Ë™ç„Åï„Çå„ÅüÔºàRyuji Hattori, 2016, 172 Ôºâ„ÄÇ„Åï„Çâ„Å´Êó•Êú¨„ÅåÔºíÔºë„Ç´Êù°„ÅÆË¶ÅÊ±Ç„ÅßÂæó„ÅüÂ±±Êù±ÁúÅ„ÅÆÊóß„Éâ„Ç§„ÉÑÊ®©Áõä„Å™„Å©„ÅÆËøîÈÇÑ„ÅåÊ±Ç„ÇÅ„Çâ„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊù°Á¥ÑÁ∑†Áµê„ÅÆËÉåÊôØ„Å´„ÅØ„ÄÅ„Ç¢„É°„É™„Ç´„Å®„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„ÅåÊó•Êú¨„ÅÆ‰∏≠ÂõΩÈÄ≤Âá∫„ÇíÊäëÂà∂„Åô„ÇãÁõÆÁöÑ„Åå„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨„ÇÇÂõΩÈöõÂçîË™ø„ÅÆË¶≥ÁÇπ„Åã„ÇâÂ±±Êù±ÁúÅ„ÅÆÊóß„Éâ„Ç§„ÉÑÊ®©ÁõäÔºàËÜ†Â∑ûÊπæÔºâ„ÅÆËøîÈÇÑ„ÇíË™ç„ÇÅ„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„Å´„Çà„ÇäÊó•Êú¨„ÅÆ‰∏≠ÂõΩ„Å∏„ÅÆÈÄ≤Âá∫„ÅåÊäëÂà∂„Åï„Çå„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨„Å®‰∏≠ÂõΩ„ÅÆ‰∫§Ê∏â„ÇÇÂæ©Ê¥ª„Åó„ÅüÔºàRyuji Hattori, 2016, 173)„Åì„ÅÆ„Çà„ÅÜ„Å™„ÉØ„Ç∑„É≥„Éà„É≥‰ΩìÂà∂„ÅØ„ÄÅÔºëÔºôÔºìÔºëÂπ¥„Å´Êó•Êú¨„ÅåÊ∫ÄÂ∑û‰∫ãÂ§â„ÇíËµ∑„Åì„Åô„Åæ„ÅßÁ∂ö„ÅÑ„Åü‰ΩìÂà∂„Åß„ÅÇ„Å£„Åü„ÄÇ

        „ÄêUnited States of America„Äë

        „ÄêChina„Äë

Country: Japan

Historical Period: 1920-1930

Governmental Regulations on Religion

  1. Please describe important governmental regulations on religion in this decade, including the Constitution‚Äôs clauses on or relevant to religion, laws on religion, laws pertinent to religion (such as on education, on military service, on public security, on civic organizations, etc.) and type your answer in the ‚ÄúYour Answers‚Äù section below.

    (Please mark the checkboxes upon completing the respective instructions):

    Please follow the rubric to organize your answer and check the box at the end of each instruction.

    1. Summary of restrictive regulations on religion in this decade (500-1,000 words). ‚òê

    2. List the names of the particular or pertinent laws in CJK (Chinese/Japanese/Korean) and English. ‚òê

    3. Bibliographical sources (in CJK and English). ‚òê

    Your Answer:

    A.

    During this period, there were still no specific restrictions on religion. Still, the scope of application of the less majesty(‰∏çÊï¨ÁΩ™, 1880Ôºâand police punishment (Ë≠¶ÂØüÁäØÂá¶ÁΩ∞‰ª§, 1908) was expanded to include policing and suppression of faith. In particular, the police punishment decree was easy to invoke, and new religions that were regarded by the police authorities as ‚Äúlicentiousness‚Äù were often restricted from expanding their organizations through frequent activities. On the other hand, the crime of impiety was first applied in the first Oomoto suppression. However, caution was required in its invocation to control the social spread and large-scale growth of new religions with doctrines that conflicted with the national constitution. (Sakamoto, 477)„ÄÄFurthermore, in 1925, Peace Preservation Law was enacted. This was intended to punish the organization and the joining of associations whose purpose was to transform the state and deny the private property system. In practice, however, it functioned broadly as a sweeping crackdown on freedom of expression.(18)

    B.

    1925 Peace Preservation Law ÔºàÊ≤ªÂÆâÁ∂≠ÊåÅÊ≥ï, Chian iji h≈ç) „Éª It was enacted to crack down on socialist and labor movements. Under this law, it prohibited any social activities which rejected the emperor-centered political system and private property. In 1928, the death penalty was added as a maximum sentence, and the law was also manipulated to suppress the labor disputes, the democratic protests, and religious organizations. During this period, there were still no specific restrictions on religion. Still, the scope of application of the decree of impiety and police punishment was expanded to include policing and suppression of faith.

    ÈôÑÂâá

    Peace Preservation Law, 1925

    1. Anyone who forms, or knowingly participates in, groups whose goal is to deny the system of private property or to change our national essence shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to ten years. Anyone who attempts to commit this crime also will be punished.

    2. Anyone who engages in discussions intended to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to seven years.

    3. Anyone who instigates actions by others intended to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to seven years.

    4. Anyone intending to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One who instigates crimes resulting in riots or violence that cause damage to property, bodily injury, or destruction of life, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to ten years.

    5. Anyone who, intending to carry out the aims of Article One, Clause One or of the crimes described in the three previous clauses, gives money, goods, or other financial benefits to others, or makes promises of such for that same purpose, shall be sentenced to prison or penal servitude of up to five years. Anyone who knowingly demands or receives such remuneration, or makes a commitment for such remuneration, shall be given the same punishment.

    6. Anyone guilty of the offenses in the five preceding articles who surrenders voluntarily to the police shall receive a lighter sentence or be exonerated.

    7. This law shall apply to anyone who commits these crimes, even if that person lives outside the regions where the law has jurisdiction.

    More detail about Peace Preservation Law, 1925

    C.

    Social Hostility toward Christianity

    1. Please describe important non-governmental hostility toward Christianity in this decade, including motivated by a particular religion, nationalism, economic and other reasons and type your answer in the ‚ÄúYour Answers‚Äù section below..

      (Please mark the checkboxes upon completing the respective instructions):

      Please follow the rubric to organize your answer and check the box at the end of each instruction.

      1. Summary of the social hostility in this decade, including conflicts and cooperation of multiple religions (500-1,000 words). ‚òê

      2. Bibliographical sources in CJK and English. ‚òê

      Your Answer:

      After the death of founder Nao Deguchi in 1918, Oninsaburo Deguchi became the center of the organization, and from around that time onward, it expanded throughout Japan. In addition, people related to the navy and the army joined the Omoto, and their activities attracted the attention of the judicial authorities. In 1919, investigations began into the Imperial Way of Oomoto-kyo (495) The Kyoto Prefectural Police sent in police to gather information, and on February 11, 1921, Onizaburo and three others were arrested. At the trial, the charges against Deguchi Oninsaburo and Asano Kazaburo included violations of the Penal Code for disrespect and the Newspaper Law. The charges against Deguchi and Asano were enumerated. In the section on disrespect, it was stated that they had committed an act of gross desecration and disrespect of the Emperor‚Äôs dignity by writing articles in a newspaper published by the Omoto that delusionally commented on the Emperor‚Äôs actions, violated the wisdom of the Emperor, desecrated his dignity, and ignored his right to rule. Furthermore, publishing such an article violated the Newspaper Law. (496)

      1928 First Suppression of Honmichi(„Åª„Çì„Åø„Å°Á¨¨‰∏ÄÊ¨°ÂºæÂúß)

      This incident, known as the First Tenrikyo Research Association Incident, became an issue of disrespect. The Tenri Kenkyukai, which was started by Aijiro Onishi, a Tenrikyo believer, based on his interpretation, became an issue of disrespect to the emperor, and arrests continued from April 8 to August 1928. Almost 180 people were prosecuted, and 287 were exempted from prosecution. (496).

      One opportunity to consider this period regarding the relationship between Japan and U.S. Christianity is the enactment of the Immigration Act of 1924 and also anti-American sentiment among the Japanese. The law went into effect on July 1, 1924, but in June, a declaration opposing the U.S. immigration law was issued by the Japanese Christian League.When the Immigration Act of 1924 was proposed, Some of Christians in Tokyo gathered at Shigenobu Okuma‚Äôs house and sent a telegram to the President of the United States to stop this Act, pointing out that it would harm US-Japan relations(Sunakawa, 1965, vol.2, 202).

      After the Immigrant Act passage, Christians condemned it as the most severe violation of the spirit of Christianity, and on June 27, they issued a resolution jointly signed by themÔºàSunakawa, 1965,vol.2, 203)

      .

      Japan Christian Volunteers‚Äô Resolution Against U.S. the Immigration Act of 1924

      1. In the spirit of our previous efforts to prevent such legislation, we Christians unite in urging the American people to reflect on the situation and to take every opportunity to resolve it thoroughly.

      2. We Christians, who believe in racial equality for all humankind, look forward to working together with Christians worldwide to realize this ideal internationally and make further efforts in the future.„ÄÄ

      3. We Christian believers know the seriousness of our responsibility to the current times. To accomplish our mission, we expect you to promote and complete the independence of Christian churches and other Christian organizations, which has been our desire for many years.

      After the passage of this Immigrant Act, Inazo Nitobe, who had studied in the United States and was a pro-American Christian, stated that he would ‚Äúnever step on American soil again‚Äù (Sawada, 2019, 133). Many other Christians were disappointed by the passage of the Act and were concerned about the deterioration of relations with the United States.

      On the other hand, in response to such anti-American sentiment in Japan, activities were developed to solve the tension between the two countries through private cultural exchanges. It was called Friendship Dolls(Japanese friendship dolls (ÂèãÊÉÖ‰∫∫ÂΩ¢, y≈´j≈ç ningy≈ç), or Japanese ambassador dolls, and the American blue-eyed dolls (Èùí„ÅÑÁõÆ„ÅÆ‰∫∫ÂΩ¢, aoi me no ningy≈ç). About 12,000 dolls were donated from the United States in time for the Japanese Doll Festival. The movement was planned by Sydney Lewis Gulick (1860-1945), a missionary of the American Board who worked in Japan for many years. In 1925 the Committee of World Friendship among Children (CWFC) was formed. The first project of the CWFC was a goodwill activity through dolls during the Doll Festival on March 3, 1927 (Kohiyama, 2005, 57). On the Japanese side, the Ministry of Education and Foreign Affairs distributed the dolls, and the Shibusawa Foundation covered most of the expenses after Eiichi Shibusawa endorsed the project. On the other hand, 58 Japanese dolls were presented to the United States in November as ‚Äúreply dolls‚Äù led by Shibusawa. They were donated to museums and art galleries throughout the United States.IIt was mentioned that many Christian women and their traditions behind the doll exchange.(Kohiyama, 2005, 65) Since the 19th century, many American women have engaged in overseas missionary work. As an extension, those who had previously participated in such missions created and prepared doll clothing (Kohiyama, 2005,61). Thus, it is shown that American Christian women supported this activity. On the other hand, Gulick and the CWFC were careful not to indicate too much that their activities were religious. They thought that it was essential to appeal to secular groups to receive support from the wider public (Kohiyama, 2005,62). Therefore, prioritizing the dolls occurred over appeals to ease US-Japan relations, such as ‚Äúfriendship,‚Äù rather than religious connotations(Kohiyama, 2005, 63-64). Thus, the project forgot about American women and the religion behind it. It was carried out to improve US-Japan relations and benefit the country, which was the concern of Japanese male leaders(Kohiyama, 2005, 66) . Furthermore, the project was also implemented in the colonies and used to reinforce Japanese colonial policies.

        „ÄÄ„Åï„Çâ„Å´„ÄÅ‰∫î„Ç´ÂõΩÊù°Á¥ÑÔºà„ÉØ„Ç∑„É≥„Éà„É≥Êµ∑ËªçËªçÁ∏ÆÊù°Á¥ÑÔºâ„Åß„ÅØ„ÄÅÂêÑÂõΩ„ÅÆ‰∏ªÂäõËâ¶ÔºàÊà¶Ëâ¶„Å®Á©∫ÊØçÔºâÂª∫ÈÄ†„Çí10Âπ¥ÈñìÂÅúÊ≠¢„Åó„ÄÅÂêÑÂõΩ„ÅÆ‰øùÊúâÊØîÁéá„Çí„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„Å®„Ç¢„É°„É™„Ç´„ÅåÂêÑÔºï„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨3„ÄÅ„Éï„É©„É≥„Çπ„Å®„Ç§„Çø„É™„Ç¢„ÅåÂêÑ1.67„Å®Âèñ„ÇäÊ±∫„ÇÅ„ÅüÊù°Á¥Ñ„Åß„ÅÇ„Å£„Åü„ÄÇÊó•Êú¨ÂÅ¥„ÅØË≤°ÊîøË≤†ÊãÖ„ÇÑÂØæÁ±≥ÂçîË™ø„ÅÆË¶≥ÁÇπ„Åã„Çâ„Åì„ÅÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„ÇíÂèóË´æ„Åó„ÄÅ„Åù„ÅÆ‰ª£„Çè„Çä„Å´„ÄÅÂ§™Âπ≥Ê¥ã„ÅÆÂ≥∂„ÄÖ„Å´„Åä„Åë„ÇãÊµ∑ËªçÂü∫Âú∞„ÅÆÂº∑ÂåñÁ¶ÅÊ≠¢„ÇíÊèêÊ°à„Åó„ÄÅÁ¨¨19Êù°„Å®„Åó„Å¶ÊâøË™ç„Åï„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„Å´„Åä„ÅÑ„Å¶ÊúÄ„ÇÇÂ§ö„Åè„ÅÆËªçËâ¶„ÇíÂªÉÊ£Ñ„Åó„Åü„ÅÆ„ÅØ„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„Åß„ÅÇ„Çä„ÄÅ„Åì„ÅÆ„Åì„Å®„ÇÇ„Ç§„ÇÆ„É™„Çπ„ÅÆÂΩ±ÈüøÂäõ„ÅÆ‰Ωé‰∏ã„Å´„Å§„Å™„Åå„Å£„Åü„ÄÇ„Åì„ÅÆÊµ∑ËªçËªçÁ∏ÆÊù°Á¥Ñ„Å´„Çà„ÇäÁ¨¨‰∏ÄÊ¨°‰∏ñÁïåÂ§ßÊà¶„Åã„ÇâÁ∂ö„ÅèÊà¶Ëâ¶Âª∫ÈÄ†„ÅÆÁ´∂‰∫â„ÅØ‰∏ÄÊôÇÁöÑ„Å´Êäë„Åà„Çâ„Çå„Åü„ÄÇ(Ryuji Hattori, 2016, 171 )
